[1] Chapter 1: On Early Rising
[2] A young man named Jimmy Sycamore, with a face full of smiles, ran down the wide staircase of the Chimney House in three strides instead of two. He ran so fast that he collided with the stern butler, Mr. Truddwell, who was carrying a pot of freshly brewed hot coffee through the hall. Fortunately, Mr. Truddwell was quick-witted and avoided any harm.
[3] "I'm sorry," Jimmy apologized hastily. "By the way, Mr. Truddwell, am I the last one down?"
[4] "No, sir, Mr. Wade is still not down yet."
[5] "Oh," Jimmy said, and went into the breakfast room.
[6] Only the landlady was in the room. She gave Jimmy a reproachful look, which made him uncomfortable, like the feeling of seeing dead fish eyes on a fishmonger's stall. How unreasonable! Just because he had overslept and didn't come downstairs at 9:30 in the morning. It was now 11:15, and it was indeed a bit excessive. However, even so...
[7] "Mrs. Cutt, I'm afraid I got up a little late, isn't it?"
[8] "Oh, it's alright," Mrs. Cutt replied, sounding a bit downcast.
[9] In fact, Mrs. Cutt was always very uneasy when people were late for breakfast. During the first ten years of her marriage to Sir Osbert Cutt (who had not yet gained his knighthood), if Sir Osbert had been even a minute late for breakfast, he would have raged. Gradually, Mrs. Cutt began to see tardiness as the most unforgivable sin. Habits are hard to break, and she was a person of haste. Sometimes she couldn't help but wonder what great things these young people who couldn't even get up early could accomplish in the world. Just as Sir Osbert often said to reporters or someone else, "I owe my success entirely to early rising, thrift, and regular habits."
[10] Mrs. Cutt was a tall and beautiful woman, but there was always a touch of sadness in her demeanor. Her deep-set eyes were full of sorrow, and her voice was low. If someone wanted to paint an oil painting of "Rachel Weeping for Her Children" from the Bible, Mrs. Cutt would be an ideal model. If she were to perform in a musical, she would be outstanding as well – a pitiful wife who had suffered the abuse of a cruel husband, walking alone in the cold and snow.
[11] Her appearance made it seem as if she had suffered great pain in life, but in fact, Mrs. Cutt had never experienced hardship, and after marrying Sir Osbert, she had also become a woman of high status. When she was a girl, Mrs. Cutt was a happy and carefree little girl who deeply loved Osbert - a young man with ambition who worked in a bicycle shop next to her father's hardware store. They lived happily together, first renting a few rooms, then a small house. Later, their house grew larger and better, but always near "the factory" - until Sir Osbert became prominent and no longer depended on "the factory." He always liked to rent the largest and most luxurious mansions in England. "Smokestack Villa" was a place with a long history, and when Sir Osbert rented it from the Earl of Kettlem two years ago, he felt he had reached the pinnacle of his life.
[12] However, Mrs. Cutt was not very happy about this. She was a reclusive woman. Her main pastime when she was first married was chatting with "the girls" - even when the number of "girls" increased greatly, her main pastime was still chatting with the servants. Now, Mrs. Cutt had a group of maids, a grand butler-like steward, several tall male servants, a group of chattering girls helping in the kitchen, a temperamental foreign chef, and a big, lumbering housekeeper who walked around croaking - but Mrs. Cutt still felt like she was exiled to a deserted island.
[13] She sighed heavily, slowly walked out of the open French window, which greatly relieved Jimmy Seccage and allowed him to eat a few more slices of kidney and bacon.
[14] Mrs. Cutt stood on the terrace for a while, worried, and then mustered the courage to speak to McDonald. McDonald was the head gardener, and he was surveying his kingdom like a tyrant.
[15] Mrs. Cutt nervously approached him.
[16] "Good morning, MacDonald."
[17] "Good morning, Lady Cutte."
[18] He answered in the tone of a gardener's boss - a little sad but dignified - like a king at a funeral.
[19] "I was thinking... can we pick some grapes that are ripe today to make a dessert?"
[20] "They're not ripe enough to pick yet," MacDonald replied.
[21] His tone was soft but firm.
[22] "Oh," said Mrs. Kuter, summoning up courage to continue, "but I tasted one yesterday, and it was very good."
[23] McDonald stared at her, making her blush. His gaze made her feel that her impertinent behavior was unforgivable. It was clear that Lady Kutel never behaved so rudely, running around the greenhouse and picking grapes to eat.
[24] "If you had asked, Madam, we would have sent a bunch to you," McDonald said with a touch of severity.
[25] "Oh, thank you," said Mrs. Kuter. "Yes, I'll do that next time."
[26] "It's not quite the time to pick them yet."
[27] "Yes," Lady Kutta murmured, "yes, probably not. Well, never mind."
[28] McDonald remained silent, his silence conveying a sense of authority. Lady Kutta gathered her courage again.
[29] "I was wanting to talk to you about the lawn behind the rose garden. I was wondering if we could turn it into a croquet lawn. Sir Osborne enjoys playing croquet very much."
[30] "What's the harm in that?" Lady Kutta thought to herself. She had learned in her British history class that it was during a game of croquet that Sir Drake and his knightly companions discovered the Spanish Armada. This was clearly a gentleman's activity, and McDonald had no reason to object. However, she did not anticipate the stubbornness of the head gardener - he simply refused to listen to any suggestions from others.
[31] "No, we can't do that," McDonald said without agreement.
[32] He poured cold water on her, but his real intention was to coerce Mrs. Cutt into giving up entirely.
[33] "If we clean it up and… uh… trim it… and so on," she still held onto hope.
[34] "Well," McDonald said slowly, "we can do that. But then we'd have to get William over there."
[35] "Oh!" said Mrs. Cutt, puzzled. "Over there" meant nothing to her – it only vaguely reminded her of a Scottish folk song – but for McDonald, it was obviously the strongest objection.
[36] "That would be a shame," McDonald said.
[37] "Oh, yes," Mrs. Cutt said, "of course." But she quickly wondered why she had so readily agreed.
[38] McDonald stared at her intently.
[39] "Of course," he began, "if you have a request, ma'am..."
[40] He didn't finish his sentence, but the threatening tone was clear to Mrs. Cutt. She surrendered immediately.
[41] "Oh, no," she said, "I understand your meaning, McDonald. No... no... William should stay there."
[42] "I agree, ma'am," said McDonald.
[43] "Yes, yes, certainly," said Mrs. Cutt.
[44] "I believe you would agree, ma'am," said McDonald.
[45] "Oh, certainly," said Mrs. Cutt again.
[46] So, Mr. McDonald straightened his hat and turned away.
[47] Mrs. Cutt watched him as he walked away, sighing unhappily. Jimmie Sycamore, who had just finished a meal of kidney and bacon, came out onto the terrace and stood next to her. He sighed too, but with a different feeling.
[48] "What a wonderful morning it was, wasn't it?" he said.
[49] "Was it?" said Mrs. Cutt absently. "Oh, yes, probably. I didn't notice."
[50] "What about the others? Were they out on the lake?"
[51] "I think so. I mean, if they were out there, I wouldn't be surprised."
[52] Mrs. Cutt turned back to the house. Trudwell was examining the coffee pot.
[53] "Oh, dear," Mrs. Cutt said. "That gentleman hasn't…"
[54] "You mean Mr. Wade, ma'am?"
[55] "Yes, Mr. Wade. He hasn't come down yet?"
[56] "No, ma'am."
[57] "It's very late."
[58] "It is late, ma'am."
[59] "Oh, dear! I suppose he'll be down soon, Traddles?"
[60] "Yes, ma'am. Mr. Wade came down at eleven-thirty yesterday, ma'am."
[61] Mrs. Cutt looked at the clock, which was twenty minutes to twelve. She felt a little sorry for him.
[62] "You've got a lot to do, Mr. Tredwell. You have to clean up here and be ready for lunch by one."
[63] "I'm used to the way young gentlemen live, ma'am."
[64] He spoke quietly, but his disapproval was clear. It was the kind of tone a bishop might use when reprimanding a careless Turk or pagan.
[65] Mrs. Cutt's face flushed again, but the door opened just then, breaking the awkwardness. A serious-looking young man with glasses poked his head in.
[66] "Oh, Mrs. Cutt, Sir Osborne asks you to come to him."
[67] "Yes, I'll be right there, Mr. Bettman."
[68] Mrs. Cutt hurried away.
[69] Rupert Bettman, Sir Osborne's private secretary, left through the French windows in another direction, while Jimmy Cessie still sat there enjoying the view.
[70] "Good morning, ape," Jimmy greeted him. "I suppose I have to go and flatter those damn girls. Shall we go together?"
[71] Bettman shook his head and walked off along the terrace and away from the windows.
[72] Watching him disappear, Jimmy grinned. He and Bettman had been at school together, and Bettman had always worn glasses and been a serious, quiet man, but for some reason he had been given the nickname "chimpanzee."
[73] Jimmy thought, the chimpanzee was still the same old dimwit. "Life is real, life is earnest," the phrase applied to him as well as anyone.
[74] Jimmy yawned and slowly made his way towards the lake. There were three girls there, all with short hair - two with black hair and one with blonde hair. The one who laughed the most was called Helen, another was called Nancy, and the third, for some reason, was known as "Socks." They were accompanied by two of his friends, Bill Everly and Ronnie DeVille. Both were in the Foreign Service, but only to pass the time.
[75] "Hi!" said Nancy (or maybe it was Helen) "Jimmy, where's that guy called..."
[76] "You don't mean," Bill Everly said, "that Gary Wade hasn't gotten up yet? We should do something about it."
[77] "If he's not careful," Ronny DeFleur said, "one day he won't get breakfast. He'll have to settle for lunch or afternoon tea when he finally comes down."
[78] "It's disgraceful," the girl called "Socks" said, "look at Mrs. Kutef, she's so worried. She's getting more and more like a hen that can't find a nest to lay her eggs. It's too much."
[79] "Let's go get him," Bill suggested, "Come on, Jimmy."
[80] "Oh, let's do it subtly," Socks interjected. "Subtle" was a word she liked to use, almost a catchphrase.
[81] "I'm a roughneck," Jimmy replied. "I don't know how to be subtle."
[82] "Let's do it together in the morning," Ronnie suggested slyly. "Wake him up at seven, make a commotion, and wake up the whole house. Mr. Truddwell's fake mustache and teapot will fall to the ground; Mrs. Cutt will go hysterical and collapse into Bill's arms – he's strong, you know; Sir Oswald will 'ha' and his steel company stock will rise; the chimpanzee will throw a tantrum, drop his glasses on the ground, and stomp on them."
[83] "You don't understand Gary," Jimmy said. "Pouring cold water on him might wake him up, but I bet he'd just roll over and go back to sleep."
[84] "Oh, we need to come up with something more subtle than that," said Socks.
[85] "Well, what do you have in mind?" Ronnie asked directly, but no one had an immediate answer.
[86] "We need to come up with something," Bill said. "Who has the most ideas?"
[87] "The chimpanzee," Jimmy said. "Look, he's coming over, as usual, in a hurry. The chimpanzee is always the most clever, and he's been that way since he was a kid. It's a pity."
[88] Mr. Betman patiently listened to their somewhat disjointed narrative, displaying a confident expression. After hearing them out, he immediately proposed his solution.
[89] "I suggest using an alarm clock," he said decisively. "I've always used one myself to avoid oversleeping. Quietly bringing in a cup of early tea might not wake someone up."
[90] He hurried off.
[91] "An alarm clock?" Ronnie shook his head. "It would take a dozen alarm clocks to wake up Gerry Wade."
[92] "Why not?" Bill's face turned red as he spoke earnestly. "I have an idea. Let's go out to the street and buy an alarm clock for each of us."
[93] Everyone laughed and continued chatting. Bill and Ronnie went to find a car. Jimmy was sent to the restaurant to investigate, and he quickly returned.
[94] "He's there, devouring toast and jam. How can we keep him from going with us?"
[95] In the end, they decided to enlist Mrs. Kutter's help in keeping him occupied – Jimmy, Nancy, and Helen completed this task together.
[96] Mrs. Kuter looked bewildered.
[97] "A joke? You have to be careful, dear children. I mean, you don't want to scratch the furniture or break anything else. We're going to return this house next week, and I don't want the Earl of Kettleham to think…"
[98] At this point, Bill returned from the garage, interrupting, "Don't worry, Mrs. Kuter. Bunty Brent, the Earl of Kettleham's daughter, is my good friend. Besides, she won't mind too much—not at all! Leave it to me. And we won't damage anything. This won't make any noise."
[99] "Very subtle," the girl named Socks added.
[100] Mrs. Kuter walked dejectedly along the terrace, and just then Gerry Wade emerged from the restaurant. Jimmy Sassig was a young man with fair skin and a baby face, while Gerry Wade appeared even fairer and more adorable. However, his face showed no expression; instead, Jimmy seemed much more cunning.
[101] "Good morning, Mrs. Küttel," Gerry Wade said, "Where is everyone?"
[102] "They went to the Bessing Mall," Mrs. Küttel replied.
[103] "What for?"
[104] "Probably to play a joke," Mrs. Küttel said in her usual low, melancholy voice.
[105] "Seems a bit early to be playing jokes," Mr. Wade said.
[106] "It's not that early anymore." said Mrs. Cutt, sounding slightly annoyed.
[107] "I must have overslept." said Mr. Wade, being candidly charming, "It's strange, though, I always seem to be the last one to wake up, no matter where I am."
[108] "That is quite peculiar." said Mrs. Cutt.
[109] "I have no idea why that is." said Mr. Wade, thinking for a moment, "I can't figure it out, but I'm sure of it."
[110] "Why don't you get up earlier?" suggested Mrs. Cutt.
[111] "Oh!" exclaimed Mr. Wade, surprised at the simplicity of the suggestion.
[112] "Oswald said many times to me," Mrs. Cutt went on enthusiastically, "that there is nothing more conducive to a young person's progress than punctuality."
[113] "I know that," said Mr. Wade. "In town, I had to do the same. I mean, I had to be at the Foreign Office by eleven in the morning. Don't think I'm a lazybones, Mrs. Cutt. Ah, the flowers on the other side of the flowerbed are so beautiful. I can't name them, but we have some at home... those pale purple ones, what are they called? My sister is very fond of gardening."
[114] Mrs. Cutt brightened up immediately, but the pang of disappointment in her heart began to simmer again.
[115] "How about your gardener at home?"
[116] "Oh, just one gardener, a bit senile and not very knowledgeable. You have to tell him what to do, and he'll do it. But, isn't that good enough?"
[117] Mrs. Cutler agreed profoundly, her deep tone enough to make her a skilled emotional actress. They began discussing the gardener's various evil deeds.
[118] Meanwhile, the group going to the Basing Mall made good progress. They rushed into the mall and bought so many clocks, leaving the store owner bewildered.
[119] "I wish Bondell were here," Bill muttered. "You know her, Jimmy? You'd like her. She's good at sports and very smart. Do you know her, Ronnie?"
[120] Ronnie shook his head.
[121] "Don't know? How did you mix it up? She's really amazing."
[122] "Be a little subtle, Bill," said Socks. "Don't talk about girls anymore. We have to do our job."
[123] Mr. DeGroatroyd was the owner of the store and immediately greeted the customers when they arrived.
[124] "To tell you the truth, Miss, I don't think you should buy this 7/11 type - of course, this type is also good. I'm not trying to belittle it, but I strongly recommend you buy the 10/6 type. It's worth the extra money, reliable quality, and it keeps good time. I don't want you to buy it and then…"
[125] It was clear that if they didn't interrupt Mr. DeGroatroyd as they would a dripping faucet, he would go on talking forever.
[126] "We don't want reliability," said Nancy.
[127] "Just as long as it lasts a day," Helen added.
[128] "Make it loud," said the sock.
[129] "We want…" Bill didn't finish his sentence because Jimmy, who knew a bit about mechanics, had already adjusted the alarm clocks' wake-up times.
[130] In the next five minutes, the whole store echoed with the deafening alarm clock sounds.
[131] Finally, they selected the six loudest ones.
[132] "I'll tell you," Ronnie said excitedly, "I'm going to buy one for the chimpanzee. It was his idea, and it wouldn't be right if he didn't join in. He counts as one."
[133] "Right." said Bill. "I'll buy one for Mrs. Kut, too. The more the merrier. And, her task is quite heavy, she might be gossiping with that little Gerry boy."
[134] At this moment, Mrs. Kut was indeed telling Gerry a story about MacDonald and a pretty girl, and she was enjoying it.
[135] The alarms were packaged and paid for. Mr. Dugatroyd looked after the departing car, his face showing confusion. These upper-class young people were really up to something, but it was hard to understand why they did it. He sighed in relief and turned to attend to the next customer, a minister's wife who wanted to buy a new-style tea kettle that doesn't drip.
[136] Chapter 2: The Alarm Clocks
[137] "Where should we put the alarm clocks?"
[138] Dinner was over, and Mrs. Cutt had been assigned another task. Osborne-Smith's sudden suggestion of bridge had saved her from it. Perhaps the word "suggestion" was not quite appropriate, as Osborne-Smith was already one of "our industrial giants," and his ideas were quickly adopted by those around him.
[139] Rupert Bettman and Osborne-Smith paired up, while Mrs. Cutt and Gerry Wade teamed up. The arrangement was perfect. Osborne-Smith was an excellent bridge player, as he was at everything else. He liked a partner who could keep up with him; Bettman was flawless on the bridge table, just as he was as a secretary. They both concentrated intently on their cards, occasionally shouting "two no trump," "double," "three hearts," while Mrs. Cutt and Gerry Wade appeared relaxed and casual, and Wade always concluded each hand with the words, "Hey, partner, you played great." His tone of voice was very flattering to Mrs. Cutt.
[140] The others should have been dancing in a large ballroom to the sound of the radio, but instead, they were gathered in Gerry Wade's bedroom, where the sound of the alarm clocks and the suppressed laughter of the girls filled the air.
[141] "Under the bed," Jimmy answered Bill's question.
[142] "What time should it be set for?" I mean, what time should the alarm go off? Should they all go off at the same time, or one after the other?"
[143] This question sparked a heated debate. One side argued that for a heavy sleeper like Gerry Wade, all eight alarms must go off at the same time. The other side believed that having the alarms ring continuously would be more effective.
[144] In the end, the second opinion prevailed, and the alarms were set to go off one after the other, starting at 6:30 am.
[145] "I hope," Bill said kindly, "that this time he gets a lesson."
[146] "Listen, listen!" Socks said.
[147] The plot to hide the alarms was just getting started when an alarm signal was heard.
[148] "Shh!" Jimmy called, "Someone's coming up the stairs."
[149] Everyone panicked.
[150] "It's okay!" Jimmy said, "It's a gorilla."
[151] Mr. Bettman took advantage of the distraction to make his way to his room to grab a handkerchief. As he passed by Mr. Wade's bedroom, he paused to peek inside and then posed a simple yet practical question.
[152] "He'll hear the ticking of the alarm when he gets into bed."
[153] The conspirators exchanged glances.
[154] "What did I tell you?" Jimmy said in admiration, "Chimpanzees are very smart!"
[155] The person referred to as smart walked away without looking back.
[156] "Yes," Ronny DeVille shrugged. "Eight alarm clocks together can be quite loud. Even if Gerry is as dumb as a donkey, he wouldn't be able to ignore it, and he'd suspect something was going on."
[157] "I doubt that," Jimmy Cessio said.
[158] "What do you mean?"
[159] "That he's as dumb as a donkey as we think he is."
[160] Ronny stared at him in disbelief.
[161] "We all know Grady."
[162] "Do we?" Jimmy countered, "I sometimes wonder who looks more like a donkey than Grady?"
[163] Everyone looked at him. Ronny's face showed a serious expression.
[164] "Jimmy," he said, "you really do have a brain."
[165] "Another chimpanzee," Bill marveled.
[166] "Oh, I just thought of it on the spot," Jimmy explained.
[167] "Hey, let's not be so subtle, shall we?" Socks called out, "What about these clocks?"
[168] "The chimpanzee is back. Let's ask him," Jimmy suggested.
[169] With everyone urging him on, the chimpanzee thought hard and then came up with his solution. "We wait until he's asleep in bed, and then we sneak in and put the alarm clock on the floor."
[170] "The little chimpanzee is right again," Jimmy said. "When the time comes, we put the alarm clock down and go downstairs so as not to arouse suspicion."
[171] The bridge game continued, but with some changes. Sir Oswald was now partnered with his wife, and every time she made a small mistake, Sir Oswald would point it out in detail. Lady Cutt was graciously accepting every reprimand, but she still showed no interest in the game. She repeated several times, "I understand, dear, it's so good of you to point it out," but she still made the same mistakes next time.
[172] Gary Wade occasionally praised the chimpanzee: "Good job, buddy, you're not bad."
[173] Bill Everly and Ronnie DeVille were plotting intensely.
[174] "If he goes to bed at twelve... how much time do you think we should allow... an hour enough?"
[175] He yawned.
[176] "Strange—I usually don't want to sleep until three in the morning, but what's the matter tonight? Am I being a good child because we have to stay up late? I'm tired now."
[177] Everyone agrees.
[178] "My dear Maria," Sir Oswald says with some irritation, "I've told you so many times not to hesitate when considering whether to steal a card. You make it obvious to everyone at the table that you have a good hand."
[179] Lady Cutt could easily deflect her husband's criticism—since Sir Oswald is on the dealer's side, he has no right to comment on his opponent's play. However, she does not do so, but instead smiles gently and leans forward, giving Mr. Wade a good look at the cards in his hand.
[180] She sees that Mr. Wade has a Q and her previous unease disappears. She plays a J, wins the trick, and decides to show her hand.
[181] "Four tricks won, and the game won!" she happily announced. "It's so lucky to win four tricks!"
[182] "Lucky!" grumbled Mr. Wade, pushing his chair back and walking over to the fireplace, joining the group gathered there. "She calls that luck. That woman needs to be careful."
[183] Lady Cutt was busy gathering the coins and silver on the table.
[184] "I know I didn't play well," she couldn't hide her joy, "but my luck was really good."
[185] "You'll never be a bridge player, Maria," said Sir Oswald.
[186] "Of course, dear," said Lady Cutts, "I know that. You've always said so, but I have been trying very hard."
[187] "She has been trying, and not at all subtly," Gerry murmured. "If she can't cheat by looking at your cards, she'll just stick her head over to see."
[188] "I know you've been trying," said Sir Oswald. "The problem is you have no feel for the game."
[189] "I know, dear," replied Lady Cutts. "You've always said so. You owe me ten shillings, Oswald."
[190] "Is that so?" Sir Oswald seemed surprised.
[191] "Yes. One thousand seven hundred points, which is eight pounds and ten shillings. You only gave me eight pounds."
[192] "Oh, dear," Sir Osborne exclaimed, "it's my fault."
[193] Lady Cutler regretfully smiled at him and took the ten shillings. Although she loved her husband dearly, she did not tolerate him giving ten shillings without reason.
[194] Sir Osborne moved to a table by the wall and had a drink of whisky and soda. He became more enthusiastic and lively. By the time everyone said their goodbyes, it was half past twelve.
[195] Ronnie De Vere lived next door to Gryll Wade, so he was assigned to keep watch. At a quarter to two, he quietly knocked on everyone's door. A group of people in various pajamas and dressing gowns gathered in the hallway, and there was a rustling of slippers, low laughter, and whispered conversations.
[196] "His light went out about twenty minutes ago," Ronnie whispered in a low voice. "I thought he wouldn't turn it off. I opened his door and looked inside, he seemed to be sleeping soundly. What should we do now?"
[197] Soon, all the alarm clocks were gathered together again. Another problem arose.
[198] "We can't all fit in there, it would be too noisy. We need to send one person in, and the others will pass the alarm clocks in."
[199] There was another round of discussion about who would be the most suitable person to go in.
[200] Three girls were eliminated because they liked to giggle. Bill Everly was also excluded because he was tall and walked loudly, and he was a bit clumsy (which he strongly opposed). Jimmy Sessay and Ronnie DeVere were considered suitable candidates, but in the end, most people thought Rupert Bettman was the most appropriate.
[201] "The chimpanzee is the best choice," Jimmy agreed, "He walks like a cat and has always been that way. Besides, if Gerry wakes up, the chimpanzee will surely come up with some plausible, non-suspicious words to say."
[202] "Some subtle words," the Sock thoughtfully said.
[203] "Right," Jimmy emphasized.
[204] The chimpanzee moved quickly and carefully, opening the bedroom door and disappearing into the darkness with the two largest alarms. A few minutes later, he returned to the door, and the others handed him two more alarms. This process was repeated twice more, and finally, he emerged. Everyone held their breath and listened carefully. Gerry Wode's even breathing could still be heard faintly, but it was soon drowned out by the loud ticking of Mr. Dumplin's eight alarms.
[205] Chapter 3: The Failed Joke
[206] "Twelve o'clock!" Socks exclaimed despairingly.
[207] The joke – if it was a joke – was not successful. However, the alarm clocks still performed their duties. They rang out on time, with such vigor and enthusiasm. Ronnie DeVille jumped out of bed, half-asleep, thinking the world was coming to an end. If the people next door were startled like this, what would the people closest to the alarm clocks be like? Ronnie rushed out into the hallway, pressing his ear to the crack in the door. He expected to hear curses from within and to know what they would be. However, he heard nothing. All the alarm clocks were ticking properly, their sounds high-pitched and irritating. Another alarm clock rang, its voice hoarse and deafening, enough to make even a deaf person jump.
[208] Without a doubt, the alarm clocks faithfully performed their duties, far exceeding Mr. Dumpty's promises. However, for Gerry Wade, the chimpanzee seemed to be a formidable opponent.
[209] The conspirators were almost desperate.
[210] The group of plotters was almost desperate.
[211] "That kid isn't human!" complained Jimmy Sexton.
[212] "Maybe he thought it was a distant phone ringing and rolled over to sleep again," Helen, possibly Nancy, speculated.
[213] "I think it's too abnormal," said Rupert Betman seriously. "I think he should see a doctor."
[214] "Maybe it's a problem with his eardrum," Bill seemed sure.
[215] "Well, as far as I'm concerned," said the socks, "he might be playing a trick on us. He can't not hear it, he's just pretending not to, so we'll be disappointed."
[216] Everyone looked at Socks with admiration.
[217] "Makes sense," Bill replied.
[218] "He's just that crafty, that's what he is," Socks said. "You'll see, he'll come in late for breakfast today – just to get under our skin."
[219] It was already past noon, and everyone agreed with Socks' theory except for Ronnie DeFleur.
[220] "You forgot, I was outside when the first alarm went off," Ronnie said. "Whatever Gerry decides to do, his first reaction should be to be startled. He should be woken up. Chimpanzee, where did you put the first alarm that went off?"
[221] "Right on a small table not far from his ear," Mr. Bettman answered.
[222] "You thought of everything, Chimpanzee," Ronnie complimented. Then he turned to Bill and asked, "If it was six thirty in the morning and you heard a deafening bell ringing inches away from your ear, what would you say?"
[223] "Oh, my goodness!" Bill said. He didn't finish his sentence.
[224] "You would, of course," Ronnie said. "I would too. Everyone would. That's a normal reaction. But he didn't. So I say, Chimpanzee is right – Gri's eardrum might be damaged."
[225] "It's already 12:20," a girl said, sounding disappointed.

[382] Chapter Four - A Letter
[383] "He doesn't consider others at all, I think," said Lord Ketteman.

[447] But that night, as she sat in her bedroom in her dressing gown, watching the firelight cast her gaunt shadow, her thoughts drifted back to the cheerful yet mundane young man, Gerry Wade. [448] She glanced at the mantelpiece and remembered the rumors about the clocks.

[459] September 21st

[469] Bondel shook her head. She looked around, shivering. Gerry could be watching her right now. He died in this very room…

[474] Chapter 5: The Man Who Fell
[475] "Dad," Bondel opened the door to the Earl of Ketterle's study and peeked in, "I'm going to take the Siamese cat into town. I can't stand the monotony here anymore."

[500] Meanwhile, Bondle impatiently honked the car horn at the gate, and a child rushed out of the lodge, followed by her mother calling out to be careful of the car.
[501] "Hurry up, Kitty. The young lady is as impatient as ever."
[502] Haste was Bondel's usual style, especially when driving. She was an excellent driver, bold and confident, and would have caused many accidents if it weren't for her skill and daring.
[503] It was a sunny October day, with a clear blue sky and bright sunshine that made it difficult to keep one's eyes open. The air was filled with the rich scent of the countryside, which made Bondel's cheeks flush with excitement and passion for life.
[504] That morning, she had sent an unfinished letter from Gerry Wade to his sister, Lorna Wade at Dean's Priory, adding a few notes of explanation. The strange feeling the letter had given her earlier in the day had faded by daylight, but she still felt it needed an explanation. She wanted to talk to Bill Everesley about the details of the tragic party that had taken place.
[505] Bondel pressed the accelerator, and the Siamese shot forward. Mile after mile of road was left behind, with few other vehicles in sight and long stretches of empty road ahead of her.
[506] However, a man suddenly rushed onto the road from the side of the hedge, right in front of Bunty's car. It was impossible to brake in time. Bunty exerted all her strength to turn the steering wheel, and the Xavier swerved to the right, almost falling into the ditch by the roadside. Although extremely dangerous, fortunately, it was successful. Bunty was sure she didn't hit the man.
[507] She looked back with a feeling of nausea. The car didn't run over the man, but it seemed to have knocked him down. The man lay face down on the road, motionless.
[508] Bunty rushed out of the car and ran back. She had at most run over a wandering hen before, but she had never encountered such a serious accident. Although this accident was almost not her fault, she didn't care at the moment. The man seemed drunk, but regardless, she had hit him – she was almost sure she had hit him. Her heart was pounding, and she could hear it herself.
[509] She knelt beside the man, tremblingly flipping him over. He neither moaned nor spoke. She found that the man was young, handsome, and well-dressed, with a small mustache.
[510] There were no obvious injuries, but she was sure he was dead or dying. His eyelids trembled slightly, and his eyes half-opened, showing pleading and pain, like a dying dog. He struggled to speak. Bunty leaned over him.

[1373] Chapter 14: The Seven Clocks Meeting

[1823] Chapter Nineteen: Bondel's Adventure
[1824] Bondel Brent was a clever and imaginative girl. She knew that Bill or Jimmy would object to her joining an evening action that might be dangerous. Bondel didn't want to waste time arguing, so she had made her own plans and arrangements. Just before dinner, she glanced out the window from her bedroom and was pleased with what she saw. She knew that there were vines climbing up the gray walls of the Double-Horned Dragon Church, and the ones outside her room were particularly sturdy. For a gymnast like her, climbing would pose no problem.
[1825] So far, she couldn't find any flaws in Bill and Jimmy's arrangements. However, she felt they weren't doing enough. She didn't say this out loud but planned to handle it herself. Overall, since Jimmy and Bill were focused on the inside, Bondel decided to focus on the outside.
[1826] She gladly accepted the insignificant role that Bill and Jimmy had assigned her, but to be honest, she was a bit disdainful of these two men. She wondered how easily they could be fooled. Bill, for one, had never been considered particularly intelligent, but at least he should have known Bondel well. As for Jimmy Sessage, although not very familiar with her, he shouldn't have thought she could be so easily dismissed.
[1827] Back in her room, Bondel quickly got to work. First, she took off her evening gown and other clothes, and then, to put it simply, she started putting on her clothes again, starting with her corset. Bondel didn't bring her maid, so she had to do it all herself. Otherwise, the unsuspecting French maid might wonder why she brought riding pants but no other riding gear.
[1828] After putting on her riding pants, rubber-soled shoes, and a dark sweater, Bondel was ready for anything. She checked the time – it was only 12:30. Whatever was going to happen, it would have to wait until everyone was asleep. Bondel set her action time for 1:30.
[1829] She switched off the light and sat patiently by the window, waiting. At 1:30, she stood up, opened the window, and swung one leg over the sill. It was a nice night, cool air, and quiet, with stars but no moon.
[1830] She easily climbed down. As a child, Bondel loved to run and play in the gardens of the Smokestack Mansion with her two sisters, and she was as agile as a cat when climbing. Bondel came to a flower bed, slightly out of breath, but unharmed.
[1831] She stopped to think for a moment. She knew that the Minister of Aviation and his secretary lived on the west side, opposite where Bunder now stood. There was a terrace between the south and west sides of the house, extending to the edge of a walled orchard.

[1833] She immediately recognized him.
[1834] "Inspector Batter! You scared me!"

[1838] "Just to check," Batter replied, "if there were any unauthorized visitors."

[1845] Detective Inspector Batter looked up thoughtfully at the ivy.
